  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer16275720799145893482"><i>Miss Lonelyhearts</i><br/><br/>Miss Lonelyhearts was a newspaper reporter, so named because he had been assigned to write the agony column, to answer the letters from Desperate, Sick-of-It-All, Disillusioned. A joke at first; but then he was caught up, terrifyingly, in a vision of suffering, and he sought a way out, turning first here, then there—Art, Sex, Religion. Shrike, the cynical </span>
  <span id="freeText16275720799145893482" style="display:none"><i>Miss Lonelyhearts</i><br /><br />Miss Lonelyhearts was a newspaper reporter, so named because he had been assigned to write the agony column, to answer the letters from Desperate, Sick-of-It-All, Disillusioned. A joke at first; but then he was caught up, terrifyingly, in a vision of suffering, and he sought a way out, turning first here, then there—Art, Sex, Religion. Shrike, the cynical editor, the friend and enemy, compulsively destroyed each of his friend’s gestures toward idealism. Together, in the city’s dim underworld, Shrike and Miss Lonelyhearts turn round and round in a loathsome dance, unresolvable, hating until death…<br /><br /><i>The Day of the Locust</i><br /><br />To Hollywood comes Tod Hackett, hoping for a career in scene designing, but he finds the way hard and falls in with others—extras, technicians, old vaudeville hands—who are also in difficulty. Around him he sees the great mass of inland Americans who have retired to California in expectation of health and ease. But boredom consumes them, their own emptiness maddens them; they search out any abnormality in their lust for excitement—drugs, perversion, crime. In the end only blood will serve; unreasoned, undirected violence. The day of the locust is at hand…</span>
  <a data-text-id="16275720799145893482" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_48757338" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/48757338" />
    <a title="Fabian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1036893-fabian"><img alt="Fabian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514061293p2/1036893.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/48757338?book_show_action=true">Mar 09, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fabian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fabian" href="/user/show/1036893-fabian">Fabian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer48757338" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer86890540643470176">Miss Lonelyhearts, a novella that made the "1001 Books You Must Read" list, is a type of companion piece to Salinger's maudlin, crude, symbolic works about humanity. It is about an advice columnist who thinks he is a demi god, who ignores the troubles of everyone around him to the point of satire, who makes fun of the people he should save. Think: a lazy, lost, heartless Frasier Crane. His co workers are assholes as well. The plot takes an unexpected turn at its climax... the antihero's fate is </span>
  <span id="freeText86890540643470176" style="display:none">Miss Lonelyhearts, a novella that made the "1001 Books You Must Read" list, is a type of companion piece to Salinger's maudlin, crude, symbolic works about humanity. It is about an advice columnist who thinks he is a demi god, who ignores the troubles of everyone around him to the point of satire, who makes fun of the people he should save. Think: a lazy, lost, heartless Frasier Crane. His co workers are assholes as well. The plot takes an unexpected turn at its climax... the antihero's fate is sealed and he gets just what he deserves.<br /><br />The irreverent "Day of the Locust" is about the moral decay of Hollywood. Los Angeles is a place where "people come to die." It is with this attitude, infused in the five or so interrelated vignettes, that we discover that the lost souls are selfish, indulgent, materialistic. Tod is a set designer who is in love with Faye Greener, a Fitzgeraldesque vixen, who pretty much beds all of his friends, works hard only for her professional advancement. But the reader knows that these girls, despite their enviable and covetable beauty, are dime a dozen. There is the tragic figure of Homer, the 40 year old prepared to get healthy eating California oranges &amp; living a lax lifestyle. There are cock fights and visits to Zelsnick-like sets, there are dreamers &amp; immigrants, there is even a climax at, very fittingly, a movie premiere. Almost as if something came full circle...<br /><br />West only wrote these two, and I am glad to have heard his singular voice. Though "Locust" (oddly named) is a masterpiece, "Lonelyhearts" seems unfulfilled and too trite. They are eye-opening and remind me of why I get an eerie feeling about Los Angeles in the first place.</span>
  <a data-text-id="86890540643470176" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/574178267" />
    <a title="Steve" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/236411-steve"><img alt="Steve" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1291695844p2/236411.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/574178267?book_show_action=true">Mar 29, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steve" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steve" href="/user/show/236411-steve">Steve</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780811218221" href="/book/show/5585547-miss-lonelyhearts-the-day-of-the-locust">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer574178267" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16402104216292677228">To be honest, I was expecting something lighter. Here was the hook: Miss Lonelyhearts, an advice columnist in the early 30’s, is really a man. Sounds like a role for Jimmy Stewart at his gosh-darned chirpiest, doesn’t it? But the first few pages put a different image in mind – it was Pottersville without benefit of George Bailey. The letters in to Miss Lonelyhearts were just so bleak. Of course, it was a time when deprivations were de rigueur. Those lacking money, health, or wedded bliss had ver</span>
  <span id="freeText16402104216292677228" style="display:none">To be honest, I was expecting something lighter. Here was the hook: Miss Lonelyhearts, an advice columnist in the early 30’s, is really a man. Sounds like a role for Jimmy Stewart at his gosh-darned chirpiest, doesn’t it? But the first few pages put a different image in mind – it was Pottersville without benefit of George Bailey. The letters in to Miss Lonelyhearts were just so bleak. Of course, it was a time when deprivations were de rigueur. Those lacking money, health, or wedded bliss had very little recourse. The joke, if there ever was one, was on him. (Note: he was only ever referred to as Miss Lonelyhearts, which makes me think we’re meant to know him as a 2-dimensional everyman.) His editor knew how hopeless these advice-seekers were, so it was a cruel act on his part to assign Miss L, a man with shaky mettle and faith as it was, to the task. <br /><br />Once I replaced my rose-colored glasses with a much darker shade, I got attuned to the mood West was going for and the dystopia he envisioned. There was certainly a noir feel to it all. The book was interesting to view as an 80 year old time capsule, too. Lots of the interactions between Miss L, his editor, and various women took place in speakeasies. Fat lips were a common occurrence, and distinctions between lovemaking and rape weren’t as clear as they should have been. It was also a time when the word “gay” was used often and differently.<br /><br />We’ve all seen reviews where the place is identified as a character. In this case, it seems more appropriate to say that the zeitgeist was. West, whose real name was Nathan Weinstein, was no doubt aware of the growing anti-Semitism. These were meaner times, in general, and it was easy to see how Miss Lonelyhearts, who had above-average levels of compassion and Christian good intent to begin with could become disaffected. Miss L went several rounds against the aforementioned zeitgeist, but you’ll just have to read the novella to find out how he fared.<br /><br /><i>The Day of the Locust</i> shares a sense of alienation. The central character was Tod (the German word for dead) and the story follows him to Hollywood where his artistic ambitions are brought down a few notches as a painter of movie sets. This one was set in the Depression era, too. He falls for an aspiring actress, Faye Greener, who tells him upfront they’ll only ever be just friends because he had no pull in the movie biz. They meet an unfortunate but financially secure schlub from Iowa who was told to go West for health reasons. The guy lets Faye stay at his place which made things complicated since one of his reasons for leaving Iowa was to suppress sexual impulses. (Did West get his directions mixed up?) Anyway, this guy’s name, Homer Simpson, sent me straight to Google. As it turns out, Matt Groenig has given two answers to the obvious question, one being that it was from this character, the other being that his dad was the source. <br /><br />These characters and a memorable set of others including Faye’s dad (a former vaudevillian), a hard-drinking, quick-tempered midget, a cowboy extra in movies and the cowboy’s Mexican friend (an expert in cock-fighting) existed at the margins of Hollywood. West got to know this world as a screenwriter so it seemed to ring true. It was truth at its darkest, though. Love was a sham, glamour-seekers were deluded, and the American dream failed both for its spirit and promise of riches. <br /><br />West died in a car accident along with his wife the year after this was published. He was 37 years old. There’s no way we can know if a country lifted from The Great Depression would have lifted him, too. He would have at least been gratified to learn that this poignant latter work made the Modern Library Top 100 list as well as the one <i>Time Magazine</i> compiled. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16402104216292677228" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer8784229199202149040">Unsettling, harsh, and wildly inappropriate mixed with a blandness that could put a reader to sleep in seconds. This book has weighed on my mind in a way others haven’t in a while. My finger hovered above the two and four stars button far too long due to my struggle with content versus writing style. <br/><br/>CONTENT: I’m just floored by the amount of crude and offensive material this author covered in such a nonchalant way. Abrupt topic introductions that led me to either reread to make sure what I perc</span>
  <span id="freeText8784229199202149040" style="display:none">Unsettling, harsh, and wildly inappropriate mixed with a blandness that could put a reader to sleep in seconds. This book has weighed on my mind in a way others haven’t in a while. My finger hovered above the two and four stars button far too long due to my struggle with content versus writing style. <br /><br />CONTENT: I’m just floored by the amount of crude and offensive material this author covered in such a nonchalant way. Abrupt topic introductions that led me to either reread to make sure what I perceived as being written was in fact what he had written, or to close the book with feelings of repulsion and question if I even wanted to continue. I really struggled with weighing the then versus now, and allowing the author leeway because the times have indeed changed. BUT a dislike of a concept is sometimes universal regardless of its tolerances throughout time, and the artistic license that times gone by grant doesn’t make it something I have to be okay with. So petty me docked it a star for its various sins. <br /><br />WRITING STYLE: To write about nothing that is smothered with angst and randomly placed bits of repugnant story is oddly captivating. I alternated between feelings of boredom, irritation, amusement and disgust. The mental images his writing created were almost horror like in my mind. From a distance a pristine character all done up in powder and bows; on closer inspection a barely contained almost mechanical being riddled with cracks and smudges. So damn off-putting.<br /><br />Oh and I can’t forget: Homer. Freaking. Simpson.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_63405710" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/63405710" />
    <a title="Jimmy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1107219-jimmy"><img alt="Jimmy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1582301234p2/1107219.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/63405710?book_show_action=true">Jul 14, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jimmy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jimmy" href="/user/show/1107219-jimmy">Jimmy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1107219-jimmy?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer63405710" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14290683469599924375">If one moral prevails throughout the two novels that Nathanael West has become famous for, it would probably be that, even in the dreariest of times, people can find salvation or refuge from suffering through art. At least this is what Miss Lonelyheart's boss, Mr. Shrike informs him of as a substitute for religion. In Shrike's own words he asks "Why don't you give them something new and hopeful? Tell them about art. Here, I'll dictate: <i>Art Is a Way Out</i>". The only problem with this suggestion is </span>
  <span id="freeText14290683469599924375" style="display:none">If one moral prevails throughout the two novels that Nathanael West has become famous for, it would probably be that, even in the dreariest of times, people can find salvation or refuge from suffering through art. At least this is what Miss Lonelyheart's boss, Mr. Shrike informs him of as a substitute for religion. In Shrike's own words he asks "Why don't you give them something new and hopeful? Tell them about art. Here, I'll dictate: <i>Art Is a Way Out</i>". The only problem with this suggestion is that, when applied to West's loathsome characters, it's essentially worthless. This almost specifically applies to Miss Lonelyhearts himself, a man who is so obsessed with such an ostensible social paradox; he doesn't really care about others, but he's so driven by his own narcissistic urge to "help people", as well as his even more selfish need to avoid loneliness despite the fact that he's a misanthrope in the vein of Dostoevsky's Underground Man, that he is completely oblivious to the rather accessible happiness that is all around him. It's problematic though because most of West's characters are exactly like this. They are ne'er do well's who wear social masks of benevolence and kindness. <br /><br />Take Homer Simpson's character in <i>Day of the Locust</i>. While he isn't quite as selfish as Miss Lonelyhearts, he'll never metamorphose out of his bumbling, oafish being because his self-loathing is channeled into amorous distractions. What part of himself he neglects to address is eventually expressed in a severely questionable act of violence toward the end of the novel. Readers may feel sympathetic toward Homer for a majority of the story, but a hapless character can only be sympathetic for so long, until they are merely perceived as pathetic. <br /><br />This is, of course, an interpretive bent. It's possible that West actually felt more sympathy than disgust when sketching these characters. Contrast usually determines the rest with stories such as these. Let's consider the other characters, the obvious wretches of West's depression-era hell.<br /><br />There's Shrike, Miss Lonelyheart's boss. This is a man who knowingly lets Miss Lonelyhearts go to bed with his wife, merely so he can humiliate him in an emotionally naked situation. He's also a cocky, insensitive, boorish liar. A newspaper man; a crook making profit off of the misery of others. Then again, so does West in a way.<br /><br />Or Tod Hackett, the Hollywood scene designer in <i>Day of the Locust</i>, a man who was clearly modeled after West himself, maybe not a screenwriter but someone trying to make his way in the entertainment industry all the same (West worked for RKO Radio Pictures, and penned thirteen scripts in his lifetime). Hackett, a man of some moral conviction, slowly degenerates into someone inundated by an uncontrollable desire for Faye Greener, an actress, in a way, the symbol of everything that he seems to hate about Hollywood, and all of Southern California for that matter. We can even see his tolerance for Homer Simpson eventually become replaced by hatred and contempt, albeit at that point Homer's mind has pretty much left the world of reason and sanity. <br /><br />So then maybe it's the sideshow-like characters of West's bleak universe that really matter; the grotesqueries, the one's that we initially ignored because we felt that their flaws and shortcomings were far too despicable and obvious. If the brief character sketches given here offer anything though, it's that nobility is absent and goodness is pretty much relative, if even existent in these two stories. It works though. Both of West's masterpieces (and they are in many ways) waste no time at driving these ideas home. He was so ruthless in his depiction of the madness of his times, that even he probably understood that he would be included in this world when critics looked back at his biography and his stories. Although, this sort of image probably concerned him as much as the concept of pride, a notion that has no meaning in West's work. <br /><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="14290683469599924375" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  <td colspan="2">

<div id="review_45967322" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/45967322" />
    <a title="Jennifer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1748244-jennifer"><img alt="Jennifer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1320243812p2/1748244.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/45967322?book_show_action=true">Feb 10, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jennifer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jennifer" href="/user/show/1748244-jennifer">Jennifer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1748244-jennifer?shelf=2009">2009</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer45967322" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13860094868842037667">I re-read these two short novels in a fit of sentimentality. <br/><br/>When I was a sophomore at USC, I took an American Literature class, ENGL 263. Taught by a man named Gustafson, this was my only venture into "properly" studying books written on this continent. During my undergraduate years, I really concentrated in stuffy, dead English writers. Every day, we'd dutifully appear for class, and he would show up a few minutes late, looking like he had just spent all night running around in a tizzy. (He ha</span>
  <span id="freeText13860094868842037667" style="display:none">I re-read these two short novels in a fit of sentimentality. <br /><br />When I was a sophomore at USC, I took an American Literature class, ENGL 263. Taught by a man named Gustafson, this was my only venture into "properly" studying books written on this continent. During my undergraduate years, I really concentrated in stuffy, dead English writers. Every day, we'd dutifully appear for class, and he would show up a few minutes late, looking like he had just spent all night running around in a tizzy. (He had young children, so that was probably an accurate guess.) I remember his frenetic energy - he never seemed to have a proper lesson plan, and he liked to throw in quotes from Bob Dylan and Joni Mitchell into all of his commentary. I liked the class a lot, despite the lack of structure. And I really enjoyed the reading list - it's what made me start thinking of Los Angeles as more than just miles of concrete and smog. We read <i>Day of the Locust</i>, a novel about the people who aspired to be part of the early movie industry, and afterwards, I devoured the rest of West's writing on my own time. <br /><br />That was a very long introduction for something that has very little to do with the book! Anyway, so I was feeling forlorn at the beginning of February. I went on a walk in my grimy downtown neighborhood, and the combination of my mood, the weather, and the old buildings reminded me of West's work. His characters are spare... and doomed. His descriptions border on surrealism. Do I like the people in these books? Not at all. They're selfish and mean and stupid. But there's a thread of black humor throughout.<br /><br />This is definitely not easy reading. While short, I had to pace myself to keep from missing things. But I'm glad I re-read them. It had been too long since I had visited these old friends. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13860094868842037667" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_53319186" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/53319186" />
    <a title="Josh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/868037-josh"><img alt="Josh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1241875686p2/868037.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/53319186?book_show_action=true">Apr 20, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Josh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Josh" href="/user/show/868037-josh">Josh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer53319186" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12908935747398981754">West is a prophet of the 20th century American wasteland, with one crucial difference: whereas Isaiah's wheel within a wheel is evidence of god's presence and mystery, West's trembling metaphors sing only Absence, Absence. False signs in other words, like whoopee cushions that someone (God, we hope, or think we hope) has left under the various sofa-cushions of human existence. But what is the difference between a sign and a false sign? I wonder that while reading the various movie-related descri</span>
  <span id="freeText12908935747398981754" style="display:none">West is a prophet of the 20th century American wasteland, with one crucial difference: whereas Isaiah's wheel within a wheel is evidence of god's presence and mystery, West's trembling metaphors sing only Absence, Absence. False signs in other words, like whoopee cushions that someone (God, we hope, or think we hope) has left under the various sofa-cushions of human existence. But what is the difference between a sign and a false sign? I wonder that while reading the various movie-related descriptions in Day of the Locust, all of which are magisterial, like slow-motion captures of horses running, hummingbirds, bullets exploding apples. West's prose is a sharp and pitiless substance. It creeps like tar over the drowning prehistorics of 1930s Hollywood, leaving no spot uncovered. And I guess that's what I love, and hate, about him. No softness. You cannot imagine anything escaping; but a Holocaust perpetuated against lego men (that is, against characters who we feel are being set up only to be knocked down, the game weighted unfairly) ends up being, not terrible (or, not only terrible), but funny: as if god appeared as some sort of gigantic praying mantis or clown, and yet critically did not realize that this was, perhaps, a less than dignified form to take. Like god's the one who doesn't get it. I feel contorted. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12908935747398981754" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_632195121" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/632195121" />
    <a title="Bruce" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2527793-bruce"><img alt="Bruce" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1289004291p2/2527793.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/632195121?book_show_action=true">Jun 03, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bruce" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bruce" href="/user/show/2527793-bruce">Bruce</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer632195121" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15542706544660532134">Like all black humor, these works are informed by a serious and intensely troubled view of man's existence -- specifically our contemporary existence where advertising tries to sell something -- a shaving lotion, a film, vitamins -- by molding our ideas of what we should be, physically, emotionally and spiritually. But <i>Miss Lonelyhearts</i> and <i>The Day of the Locust</i> also contain considerable realism amidst their raucous and hilarious satire, and the two don't sit well beside each other. To give one </span>
  <span id="freeText15542706544660532134" style="display:none">Like all black humor, these works are informed by a serious and intensely troubled view of man's existence -- specifically our contemporary existence where advertising tries to sell something -- a shaving lotion, a film, vitamins -- by molding our ideas of what we should be, physically, emotionally and spiritually. But <i>Miss Lonelyhearts</i> and <i>The Day of the Locust</i> also contain considerable realism amidst their raucous and hilarious satire, and the two don't sit well beside each other. To give one example, at the beginning of <i>Miss Lonelyhearts</i>, a girl writes to an advice column in a newspaper:<br /><br /><blockquote>I am sixteen years old now and I don't know what to do and would appreciate it if you could tell me what to do. When I was a little girl it was not so bad, but now I would like to have boy friends like the other girls and go out on Saturday nites, but no boy will take me because I was born without a nose--although I am a good dancer and have a nice shape and my father buys me pretty clothes.</blockquote><br />There are several letters like this throughout the story which spur much soul-searching by the protagonist, but they are ultimately not the author's real concern, any more than Gulliver amongst the Lilliputans was for Jonathan Swift, and thus the very real and intense empathy generated by the characters' plight is thwarted and left by the roadside.<br /><br />Apart from this esthetic fork in the road, West's narrative in both works is captivating, and certain metaphors and extended analogies are breathtakingly audacious. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15542706544660532134" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_101687841" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101687841" />
    <a title="rachel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2499261-rachel"><img alt="rachel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572660600p2/2499261.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/101687841?book_show_action=true">May 07, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="rachel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="rachel" href="/user/show/2499261-rachel">rachel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2499261-rachel?shelf=this-is-america">this-is-america</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2499261-rachel?shelf=2010">2010</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2499261-rachel?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2499261-rachel?shelf=wild-wild-west">wild-wild-west</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer101687841" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer651063278946758564">I didn't "really like" reading this book. <br/><br/>It is so very world-weary, its beauty melancholy and even mundane. <i>Miss Lonelyhearts</i>' eponymous character tries to beat the despair of reading people's problems for a living and...sort of, almost, succeeds. In <i>The Day of the Locust</i>, men and women flock to Hollywood seeking The Prize (be it a beautiful woman, fame, or simply an improved quality of life eating tropical fruits under palm trees); complications arise. It's mostly a downer. <br/><br/>But it's not comple</span>
  <span id="freeText651063278946758564" style="display:none">I didn't "really like" reading this book. <br /><br />It is so very world-weary, its beauty melancholy and even mundane. <i>Miss Lonelyhearts</i>' eponymous character tries to beat the despair of reading people's problems for a living and...sort of, almost, succeeds. In <i>The Day of the Locust</i>, men and women flock to Hollywood seeking The Prize (be it a beautiful woman, fame, or simply an improved quality of life eating tropical fruits under palm trees); complications arise. It's mostly a downer. <br /><br />But it's not completely a downer, because Nathanael West is so damn funny! Observe:<br /><br /><blockquote>When he had finished his second cup of scalding coffee, it was too late for him to go to work. But he had nothing to worry about, for Shrike would never fire him. He was too perfect a butt for Shrike's jokes. Once he had tried to get fired by recommending suicide in his column. All that Shrike had said was, 'Remember, please, that your job is to increase the circulation of our paper. Suicide, it is only reasonable to think, must defeat this purpose.'</blockquote><br /><br />(Hopefully this excerpt is not only funny to people who are clinically depressed.)<br /><br />If I had the space, I would quote the entire book. It's that good. West's writing is so perceptive and clever, without being too overt. You have to read it slowly. Every sentence is worthy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="651063278946758564" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_155954286" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/155954286" />
    <a title="gaby" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/130698-gaby"><img alt="gaby" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1431241507p2/130698.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/155954286?book_show_action=true">Mar 22, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="gaby" class="user" itemprop="url" name="gaby" href="/user/show/130698-gaby">gaby</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/130698-gaby?shelf=los-angeles">los-angeles</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer155954286" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5409795121865534741">"Violence in America is idiomatic." Nathanael West (Nathan Weinstein)<br/><br/>Reading West is to be struck, as in the face, again and again by his visceral sexual violence. It's frustrating but not surprising that the main literary legacy of West is a more generic brutality -- without acknowledgment that much of that violence is sexual in nature and theme. This shines brightest in Day of the Locust, where the very West-ian Homer Simpson (could it be a coincidence????) struggles hourly as though sex was a</span>
  <span id="freeText5409795121865534741" style="display:none">"Violence in America is idiomatic." Nathanael West (Nathan Weinstein)<br /><br />Reading West is to be struck, as in the face, again and again by his visceral sexual violence. It's frustrating but not surprising that the main literary legacy of West is a more generic brutality -- without acknowledgment that much of that violence is sexual in nature and theme. This shines brightest in Day of the Locust, where the very West-ian Homer Simpson (could it be a coincidence????) struggles hourly as though sex was a virus in him, struggles to keep it dormant, plagued with a chronic worry that it might break out and crush the wholeness of a girl "like an egg in the palm of his hand." Elsewhere, our narrator Tod fantasizes about rape while eating a steak dinner, musing over "A feeling, already, of what it would be like to push her down." This violence and obsession is foreshadowed in Miss Lonelyhearts, who "buries his face like a hatchet into her neck," but it's a more subdued thing in that novella. It blossoms in full force in Day of the Locust as a steady, ugly compulsion - the will to injure women, to rape, molest -- it becomes clear in Day of the Locust that it was something more than an occasionally ugly turn of phrase. I understand, of course, that there really was a legitimate metaphor in this obsession -- Los Angeles, and particularly Hollywood, invites dishonor in that way; its inhabitants feel a compulsion to seek its attention by any means necessary. But to discuss West without acknowledging the obvious dimension of gender politics is to do a disservice to history and to his work. It wasn't just a metaphor -- and even if it was, we might as well unpack it.<br /><br />The other stars of these books are their cities -- New York in Miss Lonelyhearts and Los Angeles in Day of the Locust. New York is a frantic maze of stairs and skyscrapers; but Los Angeles stretches out lazily across the desert. West writes of Los Angeles at a time when there was a real symbiosis between the city itself and Hollywood - both were lawless and lovely then, coarse and brutal; they shared an easy economy between mechanical lenses and streets; to see one was to see the other. Things have changed -- the two have not quite grown together. A bitterness, yes, an ennui has come to overshadow the luster. But it's something more -- perhaps it's just that the city has gotten too big to be congruent with Hollywood. Too big, too diverse. The lawlessness is disconnected, or more diffuse. You can still catch the light just right sometimes, in the corners of the Hollywood Hills or downtown. But the two have outgrown each other; nothing left but memories and innuendo. I love reading about Old Los Angeles - and there's just no one who wrote about it better than West.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5409795121865534741" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14042192584314678279">i've always loved a black comedy. and this one always has the ability to make me want to burst out into cackles and cry for three days at the same time.<br/><br/>whenever i meet someone who hasn't read this yet, i'm shocked. it seems like it should be required reading for life. <br/><br/>some of this is hard to take - the plight of miss lonelyhearts and his conflicts with the human condition, misery and religion would be unbearable to read if he had a real name. <br/><br/>miss lonelyhearts isn't a likable man. nothing pretty</span>
  <span id="freeText14042192584314678279" style="display:none">i've always loved a black comedy. and this one always has the ability to make me want to burst out into cackles and cry for three days at the same time.<br /><br />whenever i meet someone who hasn't read this yet, i'm shocked. it seems like it should be required reading for life. <br /><br />some of this is hard to take - the plight of miss lonelyhearts and his conflicts with the human condition, misery and religion would be unbearable to read if he had a real name. <br /><br />miss lonelyhearts isn't a likable man. nothing pretty happens here. but as pathetic and sad as he is, there is value in exploring just how shitty the human condition can be and just how bad things can get.<br /><br />the way west deftly turns sex, violence and misery on their heads - since they are viewed through the lens of this sort of paralyzed man - isn't just admirable. it's something i notice in books that followed for years after - a strain of blackness that seems to start with this book and then wind through countless other authors.<br /> </span>

A.), as well as finding the characters interesting for the most part, although The Cowboy and The Mexican in the final chapters made the ending drag out.   <span id="freeText6343468991588583466" style="display:none">As West is considered a "name" writer, thought I'd try this one.<br /><br />Read <i>Day of the Locust</i> first, which turned out to be a good idea. Liked it a lot for its sense of time and place (1930's L. Moreover, there wasn't a strong setting of Depression-era New York. By the end, I didn't have an idea of what I'd read for certain, and wasn't much bothered by that. Between 2 and 3 stars.<br /><br />Now I can say I've read West.</span>

<div id="review_2097413092" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2097413092" />
    <a title="Eileen Ying" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15932979-eileen-ying"><img alt="Eileen Ying" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594353641p2/15932979.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2097413092?book_show_action=true">Aug 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eileen Ying" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eileen Ying" href="/user/show/15932979-eileen-ying">Eileen Ying</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780811218221" href="/book/show/5585547-miss-lonelyhearts-the-day-of-the-locust">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2097413092" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18396651888506239680">I picked this book out years ago at The Book Thing in Baltimore because I thought the cover looked charming. I still see it all the time in indie bookstores. If you look at its Goodreads reviews, you'll see that they are overwhelmingly positive; Nathanael West definitely has a bit of a cult following. BUT if you look a little closer, you'll realize that most of these 4- and 5-star reviews are made by intellectual-looking white men. You know the type. I think this says a lot about what sort of no</span>
  <span id="freeText18396651888506239680" style="display:none">I picked this book out years ago at The Book Thing in Baltimore because I thought the cover looked charming. I still see it all the time in indie bookstores. If you look at its Goodreads reviews, you'll see that they are overwhelmingly positive; Nathanael West definitely has a bit of a cult following. BUT if you look a little closer, you'll realize that most of these 4- and 5-star reviews are made by intellectual-looking white men. You know the type. I think this says a lot about what sort of novel(la)s <i>Miss Lonelyhearts</i> and <i>The Day of the Locust</i> are. <br /><br /><u>Miss Lonelyhearts</u><br />I honestly don't remember much of this. Read most of it in ten-minute increments right before I went to bed, the usual deal. It was strange but not particularly memorable. I do recall loathing most of the characters. The concept is interesting: "Miss Lonelyhearts" writes an advice column for the New York <i>Post-Dispatch</i>. The letters he receives are often genuinely tragic, but the fact that they are sent to a faceless and facetious advice-giver renders them caricatures rather than real, affecting pleas. However, I found West's execution a little heavy-handed; he's a clever writer, but none of his characters -- not even Miss Lonelyhearts -- feel convincing. <br /><br /><u>The Day of the Locust</u><br />This is just a gross book. First off, I am so tired of reading Fitzgerald-esque novels about <i>poor young men</i> who are Deep and Discerning and Good People at heart, but are also kind of horrible bums because they're down on their luck, the world is a horrible and cruel place, they are manipulated by callous women, etc. etc. Second, the story is filled with rapey scenes and racist, sexist rhetoric. On several occasions, our ~relatable~ protagonist Tod Hackett thinks things like, "If only he had the courage to wait for [Faye] some night and hit her with a bottle and rape her" (174). Faye, of course, is the femme fatale who Tod later concludes is just a plain "whore" -- platinum blonde and maximally devious and ALWAYS pictured through a sexualized male gaze and somehow pure and profane at the same time. There are also a bunch of not-so-subtle racial jabs, mostly in the form of conversational remarks but also in the form of Miguel, the novel's only non-white character who conveniently takes on the mantle of lustful-sexual-predator-who-lacks-the-restraint-that-our-good-white-people-have. If you can't tell, I'm SO not into it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18396651888506239680" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review2097413092">

<div id="review_821327946" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/821327946" />
    <a title="Jennifer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83139-jennifer"><img alt="Jennifer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505315164p2/83139.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/821327946?book_show_action=true">Jan 12, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jennifer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jennifer" href="/user/show/83139-jennifer">Jennifer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83139-jennifer?shelf=owned">owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83139-jennifer?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83139-jennifer?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83139-jennifer?shelf=bookslut-100-books">bookslut-100-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer821327946" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17895845586910714841">I rebelled and struggled against reading these two stories, and had to force myself to press on nearly all the way through. In the end I found some redemption/value, but very little of the experience was anything I'd call enjoyable. <br/><br/>These stories are populated, by bitter, disenfranchised men who fantasize about rape as an appropriate corrective to "uppity" women. They are certainly not the source of their own ugliness -- all are struggling in a Depression-era world -- but still, the first scene </span>
  <span id="freeText17895845586910714841" style="display:none">I rebelled and struggled against reading these two stories, and had to force myself to press on nearly all the way through. In the end I found some redemption/value, but very little of the experience was anything I'd call enjoyable. <br /><br />These stories are populated, by bitter, disenfranchised men who fantasize about rape as an appropriate corrective to "uppity" women. They are certainly not the source of their own ugliness -- all are struggling in a Depression-era world -- but still, the first scene in which a group of men in a bar approvingly discuss a gang rape I wanted to throw this book across the room. This simmering hostility against women is a line that is played with throughout both stories.<br /><br />Somehow, it gets even more repulsive, but then, Oh! The mob scene at the end of <i>The Day of the Locust</i>! It's just as brutal as the rest of it, but such an effective metaphor for everything that's gone before that it shines. Pretty much the only thing that kept me from a one or two star rating. But would I recommend this book to get to that scene? Doubtful. It would have to be a highly specific set of circumstances. I mean, is it an accurate depiction of a slice of humanity in human history? It certainly feels true. Still not fun to read. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17895845586910714841" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer18387787" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer13661545429963214331">I really enjoyed "The Day of the Locusts." Any book that features a drunk dwarf in the first ten pages is ok by me. His clean, realistic style, crossing into deep psychological insight, makes me wonder what else Mr. West would have written had he not bit the bullet so early.<br/><br/>"Miss Lonelyhearts" didn't do as much for me, I must admit. Maybe I should read it again. Anyway, add Mr. West to the list of "why isn't this guy more popular?" authors. Mr. West, meet Mr. Sherwood Anderson.<br/><br/></span>

          </span>

<div id="review_3041974469" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3041974469" />
    <a title="Amy Sue" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1315384-amy-sue"><img alt="Amy Sue" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595106191p2/1315384.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3041974469?book_show_action=true">Nov 13, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amy Sue" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amy Sue" href="/user/show/1315384-amy-sue">Amy Sue</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3041974469" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7786661795245809086">Overall, the "grotesques" (as described by F. Scott Fitzgerald) in the books (two for one!) really cut through to describe the beginning of what I'd call late stage capitalism. I am, as ever, a fan of succinct wordplay, and the fact that you get so much character information and development in such short works makes these pieces masterful. I confess I almost gave these three stars because of some triggering sections, but perhaps that's the point? Now, I'll take each in turn, and below the review</span>
  <span id="freeText7786661795245809086" style="display:none">Overall, the "grotesques" (as described by F. Scott Fitzgerald) in the books (two for one!) really cut through to describe the beginning of what I'd call late stage capitalism. I am, as ever, a fan of succinct wordplay, and the fact that you get so much character information and development in such short works makes these pieces masterful. I confess I almost gave these three stars because of some triggering sections, but perhaps that's the point? Now, I'll take each in turn, and below the reviews will be another section with my favorite quotes, because I can't not pull them.<br /><br /><b>Miss Lonelyhearts</b><br /><br />In the words of Fall Out Boy (dare I!?), Miss Lonelyhearts has a "loony god complex." Women from across New York City write with truly terrible situations in which they are stuck largely because they are women. The only real options for them--to my mind--seem to be "do nothing" or "overturn the patriarchy." Cut back to Miss Lonelyhearts who can't unsee these terrible stories which haunt him, but perhaps because he is a "he" and because he is the (nonbelieving?) son of a preacher, he writes back with empty posturing. Miss Lonelyhearts is stuck in his own turmoil and I'm not sure if there is any saving to be done with anyone. An excellent, incredibly sad premise. <br /><br /><b>The Day of the Locust</b><br /><br />In the words of Bonnie Tyler, "We're living in a powder keg giving off sparks." This is the Hollywood of the 1930s. People run to this sunny dreamworld only to be met with a fun house packed with temptation and uncertainty and failure. West describes Hollywood movie lots as a dumping grown of failed dreams, and you see that through the (failed and often gory) goals of the characters. <br /><br /><b>Miss Lonelyhearts</b> Quote<br /><br />A useful theory: <i>“He sat in the window thinking. Man has a tropism for order. Keys in one pocket, change in another. Mandolins are tuned G D A E. The physical world has a tropism for disorder, entropy. Man against Nature … the battle of the centuries. Keys yearn to mix with change. Mandolins strive to get out of tune. Every order has within it the germ of destruction. All order is doomed, yet the battle is worthwhile.”</i><br /><br /><b>The Day of the Locust</b> Quotes<br /><br />A key description: <i>"He crossed the bridge and followed a little path that ended at a Greek temple dedicated to Eros. The god himself lay face downward in a pile of old newspapers and bottles.<br />From the steps of the temple, he could see in the distance a road lined with Lombardy poplars. It was the one on which he had lost the cuirassiers. He pushed his way through a tangle of briars, old flats and iron junk, skirting the skeleton of a Zeppelin, a bamboo stockade, an adobe fort, the wooden horse of Troy, a flight of baroque palace stairs that started in a bed of weeds and ended against the branches of an oak, part of the Fourteenth Street elevated station, a Dutch windmill, the bones of a dinosaur, the upper half of the Merrimac, a corner of a Mayan temple, until he finally reached the road."</i><br /><br />On 1930s Hollywood churches: <i>He spent his nights at the different Hollywood churches, drawing the worshipers. He visited the “Church of Christ, Physical” where holiness was attained through the constant use of chestweights and spring grips; the “Church Invisible” where fortunes were told and the dead made to find lost objects; the “Tabernacle of the Third Coming” where a woman in male clothing preached the “Crusade Against Salt”; and the “Temple Moderne” under whose glass and chromium roof “Brain-Breathing, the Secret of the Aztecs” was taught.</i><br /><br />Another theory: <i>Tod didn’t laugh at the man’s rhetoric. He knew it was unimportant. What mattered were his messianic rage and the emotional response of his hearers. They sprang to their feet, shaking their fists and shouting.</i><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7786661795245809086" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2007810559" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2007810559" />
    <a title="Bryan--Pumpkin Connoisseur" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16951301-bryan--pumpkin-connoisseur"><img alt="Bryan--Pumpkin Connoisseur" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602184090p2/16951301.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2007810559?book_show_action=true">Sep 24, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bryan--Pumpkin Connoisseur" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bryan--Pumpkin Connoisseur" href="/user/show/16951301-bryan--pumpkin-connoisseur">Bryan--Pumpkin Connoisseur</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16951301-bryan--pumpkin-connoisseur?shelf=boxall-read">boxall-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16951301-bryan--pumpkin-connoisseur?shelf=guardian-read">guardian-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16951301-bryan--pumpkin-connoisseur?shelf=books-i-own-and-have-read">books-i-own-and-have-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16951301-bryan--pumpkin-connoisseur?shelf=books-i-read-in-2019">books-i-read-in-2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16951301-bryan--pumpkin-connoisseur?shelf=new-directions">new-directions</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2007810559" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13781628092492378445">After recently having read Henry Miller's <i>Plexus</i>, and now Nathanael West's <i>Miss Lonelyhearts</i>, it occurs to me that 1930s America is as foreign to me as a colony on one of Jupiter's moons. I also believe there is a contemporary effort by some to portray the past in terms of today, with the objective of pointing out that people from then were just like people today. In the sense that they had the same emotional urges and drives, I might agree; but when factoring in the pressure of conformity and t</span>
  <span id="freeText13781628092492378445" style="display:none">After recently having read Henry Miller's <i>Plexus</i>, and now Nathanael West's <i>Miss Lonelyhearts</i>, it occurs to me that 1930s America is as foreign to me as a colony on one of Jupiter's moons. I also believe there is a contemporary effort by some to portray the past in terms of today, with the objective of pointing out that people from then were just like people today. In the sense that they had the same emotional urges and drives, I might agree; but when factoring in the pressure of conformity and the relative ignorance of psychology, I think the case can be made that people then were nothing like people now. Of course, some wag writing a hundred years from now will probably say the same thing about us, here in the opening decades of the 21st Century. "What a bunch of maroons!" they might say. <br /><br />I've now read all four of West's novels--I found <i>Balso Snell</i> almost unreadable, <i>A Cool Million</i> sophmorically satirical, <i>Day of the Locust</i> good but not great, and <i>Miss Lonelyhearts</i> fantastic, though fantastically bleak. <i>Miss Lonelyhearts</i> could not be written today, though I don't think it's out-of-date. Strangely enough, it's frank about things that people are too embarassed to write about today. It's the closest thing to Dostoevski that I know of that's come out of America. <br /><br /><i>Day of the Locust</i> verges on the same territory, though it takes a while to get there. Plus, it's too concerned with satirizing the mentality that can create a place like 1930s Hollywood. <br /><br />The characters in both stories seem pschologically alien to me--I think that might put a lot of readers off. And they also reflect attitudes toward women and minorities that are inherently offensive, but in this case, I believe West means us to feel that. Intense and disturbing--readers who are coming to these novels with a conception of Fitzgerald or Hemingway or Frank O'Hara in mind (West's contemporaries) will likely be disappointed; turned-off. West seems to carve out his own ground here.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13781628092492378445" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3347149346" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3347149346" />
    <a title="Matthew Appleton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/57003346-matthew-appleton"><img alt="Matthew Appleton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578773686p2/57003346.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3347149346?book_show_action=true">May 20, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew Appleton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew Appleton" href="/user/show/57003346-matthew-appleton">Matthew Appleton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099573166" href="/book/show/13536869-the-day-of-the-locust-and-miss-lonelyhearts">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3347149346" class="readable"
              >

<div id="review_17220490" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/17220490" />
    <a title="tENTATIVELY, cONVENIENCE" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/534016-tentatively-convenience"><img alt="tENTATIVELY, cONVENIENCE" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442481292p2/534016.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/17220490?book_show_action=true">Mar 07, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="tENTATIVELY, cONVENIENCE" class="user" itemprop="url" name="tENTATIVELY, cONVENIENCE" href="/user/show/534016-tentatively-convenience">tENTATIVELY, cONVENIENCE</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/534016-tentatively-convenience?shelf=literature">literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer17220490" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14658672047451111393">According to the back cover: "Nathanael West died almost unknown in 1940" - fairly young in a car crash. "Miss Lonelyhearts" is about a newspaper columnist who gets emotionally sucked into the dilemmas of the people who write in to him. A novel of conscience, set in an often conscienceless profession. "The Day of the Locust" is a critique of Hollywood - later made into a Hollywood movie. I'm 'reviewing' his 4 novels here out of my usual alphabetical order that I'm working thru my lit section in </span>
  <span id="freeText14658672047451111393" style="display:none">According to the back cover: "Nathanael West died almost unknown in 1940" - fairly young in a car crash. "Miss Lonelyhearts" is about a newspaper columnist who gets emotionally sucked into the dilemmas of the people who write in to him. A novel of conscience, set in an often conscienceless profession. "The Day of the Locust" is a critique of Hollywood - later made into a Hollywood movie. I'm 'reviewing' his 4 novels here out of my usual alphabetical order that I'm working thru my lit section in b/c I just read this in Lewis Yablonsky's great bk "Robopaths" last nite: <br /><br />Literary works abound with descriptive appraisals of the condition of "common people," their proclivity for ahuman acts, and their general simmering hostility. One perceptive literary analyst of this genre of the "silent majority" of robopaths was the brilliant novelist, Nathanael West, who revealed some dimensions of the problem. In the late thirties he came to Hollywood and trained his literary camera not on the movie studios or the stars, but on the "common people" who, as West's central character in The Day of the Locust states, "came to California to die." West, through this character, wrote: <br /><br />"All their lives they had slaved at some kind of dull, heavy labor, behind desks and counters, in the fields and at tedious machines of all sorts, saving their pennies and dreaming of the leisure that would be theirs when they had enough. Finally that day came. . . . Where else could they go but to California, the land of sunshine and oranges? <br /><br />"Once there, they discover that sunshine isn't enough. . . . Nothing happens. They don't know what to do with their time. . . . Their boredom becomes more and more terrible. They realize that they've been tricked and burn with resentment. . . . Nothing can ever be violent enough to make taut their slack minds and bodies. They have been cheated and betrayed. They have slaved and slaved for nothing." <br /><br />In The Day of the Locust a central character is Tod Hackett, a young painter who is planning a painting called "The burning of Los Angeles." (Interestingly, this artisitic and literary speculation is already a reality.) [Yablonsky's bk was published in 1972 so he's presumably referring to the Watts riots of 1968(?) here] The book ends with the "living dead" masses venting their frustration and hostility in a mad riot of fire that sets off the burning of other cities throughout the country. West, like Moreno, Capek, Huxley, and Orwell, has turned out to be a seer of an incipient apocalypse nurtured by robopathic leaders and followers in contemporary social machine societies.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14658672047451111393" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2605187623" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2605187623" />
    <a title="Beth Cato" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8706020-beth-cato"><img alt="Beth Cato" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1334094415p2/8706020.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2605187623?book_show_action=true">Nov 27, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Beth Cato" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Beth Cato" href="/user/show/8706020-beth-cato">Beth Cato</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8706020-beth-cato?shelf=literary">literary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8706020-beth-cato?shelf=hollywood">hollywood</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8706020-beth-cato?shelf=research">research</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8706020-beth-cato?shelf=2018">2018</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2605187623" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3953008075575585177">This is a book about horrible people being horrible to each other.<br/><br/>These two novels (more like novellas by modern standards) are considered classics. Miss Lonelyhearts was made into multiple movies and even an opera; Day of the Locust was made into a movie and was later dubbed one of the best books of the 20th century. That's a sad statement about the century. In any case, the movie versions must have radically changed elements from the books, as they both feature bleak tones and existential dile</span>
  <span id="freeText3953008075575585177" style="display:none">This is a book about horrible people being horrible to each other.<br /><br />These two novels (more like novellas by modern standards) are considered classics. Miss Lonelyhearts was made into multiple movies and even an opera; Day of the Locust was made into a movie and was later dubbed one of the best books of the 20th century. That's a sad statement about the century. In any case, the movie versions must have radically changed elements from the books, as they both feature bleak tones and existential dilemmas and persistent sex, along with cruel characters who can't keep their pants on.<br /><br />Miss Lonelyhearts follows the titular Miss Lonelyhearts, a man who writes answers for an advice column. He mocks the people who come to him for help, while he's an even sadder sap who hangs out with men who discuss why women should be gang raped.<br /><br />The Day of the Locust takes place in 1930s Hollywood, and I was able to get some research notes out of that--thank goodness, this thing wasn't a total waste of time! Tod is a painter for a studio, but very little of the story is on actual Hollywood. Instead, Tod obsesses over a neighbor women, Faye, and repeatedly daydreams about raping her. I am not exaggerating. To quote page 107: "Nothing less than violent rape would do." Tod even gets drunk and shows up at the funeral for Faye's father, where he then tries to rape her. (Note: don't be Tod.) And yet he keeps getting turned down by her, and calls her a slut a few times, too.<br /><br />Really, these two novels remind of when I read slush for a magazine, and how so many stories were of men taking revenge on shrewish women. I rejected those stories. I reject these two novels/novellas, too. I enjoy a good anti-hero. I don't mind dark stories. But these are pretentious and obnoxious. There are female main characters in both, but all of them are regarded as sexual objects to be pinched, humped, or raped. They serve no other purpose.<br /><br />I will not subject myself to any more of West's works.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3953008075575585177" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_339328175" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/339328175" />
    <a title="Juan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1911414-juan"><img alt="Juan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1483288796p2/1911414.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/339328175?book_show_action=true">May 30, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Juan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Juan" href="/user/show/1911414-juan">Juan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780811218221" href="/book/show/5585547-miss-lonelyhearts-the-day-of-the-locust">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer339328175" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer171489819536678405">Miss Lonelyhearts is Dostoevskiana at its best. Also reminiscent of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/49552.The_Stranger' title='The Stranger by Albert Camus' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Stranger</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/32585.Hunger' title='Hunger by Knut Hamsun' rel='nofollow noopener'>Hunger</a>, both heirs to Dostoevsky's aimless, misanthropic but morally conflicted by misery, properly modern men of which the underground man, from <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17876.Notes_from_Underground' title='Notes from Underground by Fyodor Dostoyevsky' rel='nofollow noopener'>Notes from Underground</a>, is the true original. Great powerful little book.<br/><br/>Day of the Locust is a classic California novel full of wonderful little surprises, not the least of which is a supremely repressed, awkward character named Homer Simpson. Others include: cock fig</span>
  <span id="freeText171489819536678405" style="display:none">Miss Lonelyhearts is Dostoevskiana at its best. Also reminiscent of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/49552.The_Stranger" title="The Stranger by Albert Camus" rel="nofollow noopener">The Stranger</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/32585.Hunger" title="Hunger by Knut Hamsun" rel="nofollow noopener">Hunger</a>, both heirs to Dostoevsky's aimless, misanthropic but morally conflicted by misery, properly modern men of which the underground man, from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17876.Notes_from_Underground" title="Notes from Underground by Fyodor Dostoyevsky" rel="nofollow noopener">Notes from Underground</a>, is the true original. Great powerful little book.<br /><br />Day of the Locust is a classic California novel full of wonderful little surprises, not the least of which is a supremely repressed, awkward character named Homer Simpson. Others include: cock fighting, drunken cowboys, songs about smoking pot, down and out clowns, crowd surfing, L.A. riots and a scene straight out of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/51496.Dr__Jekyll_and_Mr__Hyde" title="Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde by Robert Louis Stevenson" rel="nofollow noopener">Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde</a>. Could work as a retort to the East Coast's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/250.Tropic_of_Capricorn" title="Tropic of Capricorn by Henry Miller" rel="nofollow noopener">Tropic of Capricorn</a>. Seems misery, desperation, sex and violence are the great themes of early century american fiction (Steinbeck, Faulkner, West, Miller). Was left wondering if these "people who come to California to die" are meant to be the title's "locusts", the ending would seem to point to the biblical plague interpretation.<br /><br />Not crazy about some stunts he pulls with his endings, but his books seem more important and deserving of attention than Hemingway's and perhaps even Fitzgerald's.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="171489819536678405" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
